<section class="level-three">
    <article class="entry-wrapper">
        
        <h1>FickerStealer: A New Rust Player in the Market</h1>
        <div class="meta-wrapper">
            <div class="meta-inner">
            	
<div class="meta">
    	<span class="date">July 19, 2021</span>
    	    	   			<span class="author">Ben Cohen</span>
  		  	</div>
                                <div class="share-container">        <ul class="share-item type-blogpost four">
            <li class="share-text">Share this Article</li>        <li><a class="facebook on" data-share="facebook"  href="https://www.facebook.com/sharer/sharer.php?u=https%3A%2F%2Fwww.cyberark.com%2Fresources%2Fthreat-research-blog%2Ffickerstealer-a-new-rust-player-in-the-market">Facebook</a></li>                    <li><a class="twitter on" data-share="twitter"  href="https://twitter.com/share?text=FickerStealer%3A%20A%20New%20Rust%20Player%20in%20the%20Market&amp;url=https%3A%2F%2Fwww.cyberark.com%2Fresources%2Fthreat-research-blog%2Ffickerstealer-a-new-rust-player-in-the-market&amp;via=CyberArk">Twitter</a></li>
                <li><a class="email on" data-share="email" href="/cdn-cgi/l/email-protection#eed19d9b8c848b8d9ad3ad81809a8b809acbdcde889c8183cbdcde8397cbdcdea69b8ccbdcdfc88f839ed58c818a97d3ad868b8d85cbdcde819b9acbdcde99868f9acbdcd99dcbdcde868f9e9e8b80878089cbdcde8f9acbdcdead978c8b9caf9c85cbdcdfcbdeafcbdeafa8878d858b9cbd9a8b8f828b9ccbddafcbdcdeafcbdcdea08b99cbdcdebc9b9d9acbdcdebe828f978b9ccbdcde8780cbdcde9a868bcbdcdea38f9c858b9acbdeafba86879dcbdcde8c828189cbdcde87809a9c818a9b8d8b9dcbdcde8fcbdcde808b99cbdcde878088819c838f9a878180cbdcde9d9a8b8f828b9ccbdcadcbdcde999c879a9a8b80cbdcde8780cbdcdebc9b9d9acbdcde8f808acbdcde87809a8b9c8b9d9a8780898297cbdcde808f838b8acbdcdea8878d858b9cbd9a8b8f828b9cc0cbdcdea780cbdcde9a86879dcbdcde8c828189cbdcde9e819d9acbdcadcbdcde998bcbdcde9e9c8198878a8bcbdcde8f80cbdcde8780c38a8b9e9a86cbdcde8f808f82979d879dcbdcde8188cbdcde9a86879dcbdcde808b99cbdcde9a869c8b8f9acbdcde8f808acbdcde879a9dcbdcde818c889b9d8d8f9a878180c0c0c0cbdeafcbdeaf869a9a9e9dcbddafcbdca8cbdca8999999c08d978c8b9c8f9c85c08d8183cbdca89c8b9d819b9c8d8b9dcbdca89a869c8b8f9ac39c8b9d8b8f9c8d86c38c828189cbdca888878d858b9c9d9a8b8f828b9cc38fc3808b99c39c9b9d9ac39e828f978b9cc38780c39a868bc3838f9c858b9a">Email</a></li>        <li><a class="linkedin on" data-share="linkedin"  href="https://www.linkedin.com/shareArticle?mini=true&amp;url=https%3A%2F%2Fwww.cyberark.com%2Fresources%2Fthreat-research-blog%2Ffickerstealer-a-new-rust-player-in-the-market&amp;title=FickerStealer%3A%20A%20New%20Rust%20Player%20in%20the%20Market&amp;summary=This%20blog%20introduces%20a%20new%20information%20stealer%2C%20written%20in%20Rust%20and%20interestingly%20named%20FickerStealer.%20In%20this%20blog%20post%2C%20we%20provide%20an%20in-depth%20analysis%20of%20this%20new%20threat%20and%20its%20obfuscation...">LinkedIn</a></li>        </ul>
    </div>
            </div>
        </div>
        <div class="entry">
            <p><p style="padding-bottom:40px"><img width="840" height="360" src="https://www.cyberark.com/wp-content/uploads/2021/07/Option-1.png" class="attachment-post-thumbnail size-post-thumbnail wp-post-image" alt="FickerStealer: A New Rust Player in the Market" decoding="async" loading="lazy" srcset="https://www.cyberark.com/wp-content/uploads/2021/07/Option-1.png 840w, https://www.cyberark.com/wp-content/uploads/2021/07/Option-1-300x129.png 300w, https://www.cyberark.com/wp-content/uploads/2021/07/Option-1-768x329.png 768w, https://www.cyberark.com/wp-content/uploads/2021/07/Option-1-150x64.png 150w" sizes="auto, (max-width: 840px) 100vw, 840px"></p><p>This blog introduces a new information stealer, written in Rust and interestingly named <strong>FickerStealer</strong>. In this blog post, we provide an in-depth analysis of this new threat and its obfuscation techniques, including an IDAPython deobfuscator script.</p>
<h2>Background</h2>
<p>FickerStealer is an exceptional information stealer, first seen in the wild in <strong>August 2020</strong>. This info-stealer was sold on underground forums as a MaaS (Malware-as-a-service) by a seller named @ficker (@ficker_sup on Telegram). The average price of an information stealer is $200, and it varies depending on their additional capabilities and the subscription time for the service. The price for FickerStealer cost ranges between $90 up to $900, and it only depends on the subscription time to the service.</p>
<p style="text-align: center;"><img loading="lazy" decoding="async" class="size-full wp-image-120054 aligncenter" src="https://www.cyberark.com/wp-content/uploads/2021/07/1.png" alt="Figure 1" width="1070" height="425" srcset="https://www.cyberark.com/wp-content/uploads/2021/07/1.png 1070w, https://www.cyberark.com/wp-content/uploads/2021/07/1-300x119.png 300w, https://www.cyberark.com/wp-content/uploads/2021/07/1-1024x407.png 1024w, https://www.cyberark.com/wp-content/uploads/2021/07/1-768x305.png 768w, https://www.cyberark.com/wp-content/uploads/2021/07/1-150x60.png 150w" sizes="auto, (max-width: 1070px) 100vw, 1070px"></p>
<p style="text-align: center;">Figure 1: FickerStealer post on a known Russian forum</p>
<p style="text-align: left;">Once a buyer purchases the Ficker service, an &ldquo;on-prem&rdquo; package will be provided, including the C2 server setup (called <em>Pane</em>l) and the stealer executable (called <em>Build</em>). After the purchase, the buyer is required to specify what the C2 server&rsquo;s address will be, so the malware author (the seller) will configure the malware (<em>build</em>) to communicate with the attacker&rsquo;s C2 server.<br>
One of the more surprising things about FickerStealer is that it&rsquo;s written in Rust and Assembly. Rust language isn&rsquo;t a natural choice for malware developers. While it is very fast and memory-efficient and runs on embedded devices, developers might take a longer time to learn Rust because of its complexities. The advantages of this programming language are not utilized to their fullest when building malware, except that it&rsquo;s difficult to reverse engineer it. Because of that, there are few known malware families written in Rust, so it was a bit surprising&nbsp;to find a new Rust infostealer.</p>
<p>FickerStealer has quickly gained popularity among cybercriminals on underground forums because of its attractive price and interesting and different methodology as an information stealer (we will dive into that later). The malware received many good reviews from customers. For example: <strong><em>&ldquo;I use it for targeted attacks, in a couple of months the product showed itself at its best, I recommend.&rdquo;</em></strong> It even received an excellent review from some known underground forum moderators &mdash; the dream of every malware author.</p>
<p><img loading="lazy" decoding="async" class="size-full wp-image-120062 aligncenter" src="https://www.cyberark.com/wp-content/uploads/2021/07/2.png" alt="Figure 2" width="848" height="160" srcset="https://www.cyberark.com/wp-content/uploads/2021/07/2.png 848w, https://www.cyberark.com/wp-content/uploads/2021/07/2-300x57.png 300w, https://www.cyberark.com/wp-content/uploads/2021/07/2-768x145.png 768w, https://www.cyberark.com/wp-content/uploads/2021/07/2-150x28.png 150w" sizes="auto, (max-width: 848px) 100vw, 848px"></p>
<p style="text-align: center;">Figure 2: Part of the forum moderator review (translated from Russian)</p>
<p>The malware is used to steal sensitive information, including <strong>login credentials</strong>, <strong>credit card information</strong>, <strong>cryptocurrency wallets</strong> <strong>and browser information </strong>from applications such as WinSCP, Discord, Google Chrome, Electrum, etc. It does all that by implementing a different approach than other stealers (we&rsquo;ll cover it later). Additionally, FickerStealer can function as a <strong>File Grabber </strong>and collect additional files from the compromised machine, and it can act as a <strong>Downloader</strong> to download and execute several second-stage malware.</p>
<h2>Delivery Methods &ndash; Malware Distribution</h2>
<p>We noticed that FickerStealer is primarily delivered by two different methods: <strong>Exploit kit &ndash; RigEK</strong> and <strong>Malspam &ndash; Hancitor</strong>.</p>
<ul>
<li><strong>RigEK </strong>&ndash;The victims enter a malicious/compromised website. The malicious website acts as a <em>gate</em> that can perform some checks of the user characteristics, like host machine, GeoIP, user-agent, etc. In case this type of user is targeted, the user is redirected to the RigEK landing page.<br>
The landing page scans and profiles the victim&rsquo;s browser and checks if it is vulnerable to any of the exploit kit vulnerabilities (most of them are 1-day vulnerabilities). In case the browser is vulnerable, the landing page executes the exploit to load the malware payload.</li>
<li><strong>Hancitor</strong> &ndash; Malicious spam campaigns have proven to be an efficient infection vector. The malware is delivered via targeted phishing emails with malicious macro-based documents attached. The macro drops the Hancitor loader, which then injects the final payload using process hollowing. It&rsquo;s worth mentioning that we found many samples of FickerStealer that Hancitor delivers. If you are interested in more information about Hancitor, you should check <a href="https://www.binarydefense.com/analysis-of-hancitor-when-boring-begets-beacon/" target="_blank" rel="noopener">Binary Defense&rsquo;s blog</a>.</li>
</ul>
<p><img loading="lazy" decoding="async" class="size-full wp-image-120070 aligncenter" src="https://www.cyberark.com/wp-content/uploads/2021/07/3.png" alt="Figure 3: Hancitor loading Ficker payload in a sandbox environment" width="725" height="184" srcset="https://www.cyberark.com/wp-content/uploads/2021/07/3.png 725w, https://www.cyberark.com/wp-content/uploads/2021/07/3-300x76.png 300w, https://www.cyberark.com/wp-content/uploads/2021/07/3-150x38.png 150w" sizes="auto, (max-width: 725px) 100vw, 725px"></p>
<p style="text-align: center;">Figure 3: Hancitor loading Ficker payload in a sandbox environment</p>
<h2>Technical Overview</h2>
<p>As we mentioned earlier, Ficker is written in Rust, making the analysis complex and challenging because it&rsquo;s compiled differently than C/C++ language. On top of that, the Ficker executable is also heavily obfuscated.</p>
<p>FikcerStealer&rsquo;s main objective, like all credential theft malware, is to gather as much sensitive information as possible. Before we&nbsp; dive into analyzing FickerStealer&lsquo;s features, the following are the main diffrencess between Ficker and other stealers:</p>
<ul>
<li>Ficker <strong>doesn&rsquo;t have dependencies</strong>. It doesn&rsquo;t need to download/load other DLLs like <em>dll</em>, <em>nss3.dll</em> on runtime to fulfill its stealing capabilities.</li>
<li>The <strong>communication channel with the C2 server is encrypted/obfuscated</strong> from the client-side, unlike most stealers that rely on HTTP protocol and send the data as plain text.</li>
<li>Ficker <strong>sends the stolen data after each successful stealing operation</strong> and by doing that, it doesn&rsquo;t write any logs/files to disk. Other stealers write the stolen data to some temp folder, compress it and send it as a zip file to the C2 server. In addition, Ficker sends the sensitive Mozilla browser data to the C2 server and <strong>decrypts it on the server-side</strong>.</li>
<li>The Downloader feature can download and <strong>execute several PE files, including executing malicious executables and loading DLLs</strong>.</li>
</ul>
<p>Ficker tries to make its malware more reliable and stealthier than other stealers.</p>
<p><img loading="lazy" decoding="async" class="size-full wp-image-120078 aligncenter" src="https://www.cyberark.com/wp-content/uploads/2021/07/4.png" alt="Figure 4: Malware flow" width="759" height="769" srcset="https://www.cyberark.com/wp-content/uploads/2021/07/4.png 759w, https://www.cyberark.com/wp-content/uploads/2021/07/4-296x300.png 296w, https://www.cyberark.com/wp-content/uploads/2021/07/4-150x152.png 150w, https://www.cyberark.com/wp-content/uploads/2021/07/4-100x100.png 100w" sizes="auto, (max-width: 759px) 100vw, 759px"></p>
<p style="text-align: center;">Figure 4: Malware flow</p>
<h2>In-depth Analysis</h2>
<p>As far as we know, the first Rust malware was spotted in the wild in 2016. So far, we haven&rsquo;t heard about many Rust malware families or any trend of writing malware in Rust among malware developers as we have seen lately with Golang. Most of the Rust malware we stumbled upon, like <a href="https://www.welivesecurity.com/2018/10/11/new-telebots-backdoor-linking-industroyer-notpetya/">Telebots backdoor</a>, <strong>contains Rust debug information</strong>, which makes our life easier while analyzing those Rust samples. Unfortunataley, in our case, the debug information of our FickerStealer sample is stripped out, making the analysis more difficult.</p>
<p>As you can probably guess, we began by opening it in IDA, and we noticed that there are no symbols for Rust functions (or any developer functions). In addition, we could easily tell that the sample was heavily obfuscated: all the strings are encrypted; it uses many dynamic calls; and most of the functions are just wrappers on wrappers with minor modifications.</p>
<p><img loading="lazy" decoding="async" class="size-full wp-image-120086 aligncenter" src="https://www.cyberark.com/wp-content/uploads/2021/07/5.png" alt="Figure 5: Part of Ficker&rsquo;s WinMain function" width="617" height="690" srcset="https://www.cyberark.com/wp-content/uploads/2021/07/5.png 617w, https://www.cyberark.com/wp-content/uploads/2021/07/5-268x300.png 268w, https://www.cyberark.com/wp-content/uploads/2021/07/5-150x168.png 150w" sizes="auto, (max-width: 617px) 100vw, 617px"></p>
<p style="text-align: center;">Figure 5: Part of Ficker&rsquo;s WinMain function</p>
<p>Let&rsquo;s see how we approached the task of dissecting the malware.</p>
<p>Our mindset was to first &ldquo;map&rdquo; the malware by finding its strings, as that would reveal the malware&rsquo;s functionality and allow us to make educated guesses about what a snippet of code or function does.</p>
<h3>Strings Obfuscation</h3>
<p>The problem is that all Ficker&rsquo;s strings are obfuscated, including API function names, C2 server address, mutex name, sensitive and targeted file names, etc. As mentioned, revealing those strings can shed some light on the malware capabilities and IoCs.</p>
<p>While analyzing Ficker&rsquo;s string decryption process, we found that Ficker doesn&rsquo;t decrypt all the strings at once like <a href="https://www.cyberark.com/resources/threat-research-blog/meet-oski-stealer-an-in-depth-analysis-of-the-popular-credential-stealer">Oski Stealer</a> does. It decrypts each string at a different location on runtime just before it uses it. Therefore, we had to <strong>find the string decryption function</strong> and <strong>find every call to it</strong>.</p>
<p>Also, while going over the malware, we noticed many dynamic calls, so we started debugging it. Ficker tries to hide its call to the string decryption function (<span class="cyb-inline-code-labs">string_decrypt</span>) by obfuscating the function address. It calculates the function&rsquo;s address by using different instructions and different offsets. In figure 6 (highlighted with red boxes), you can see a simple dynamic call to <span class="cyb-inline-code-labs">string_decrypt</span> using short and straightforward obfuscation (which is not the case in most calls to <span class="cyb-inline-code-labs">string_decrypt</span>).</p>
<p><img loading="lazy" decoding="async" width="309" height="153" class="size-full wp-image-120094 aligncenter" src="https://www.cyberark.com/wp-content/uploads/2021/07/6.png" alt="Figure 6: Obfuscated call to string_decrypt function" srcset="https://www.cyberark.com/wp-content/uploads/2021/07/6.png 309w, https://www.cyberark.com/wp-content/uploads/2021/07/6-300x149.png 300w, https://www.cyberark.com/wp-content/uploads/2021/07/6-150x74.png 150w" sizes="auto, (max-width: 309px) 100vw, 309px"></p>
<p style="text-align: center;">Figure 6: Obfuscated call to <span class="cyb-inline-code-labs">string_decrypt</span> function</p>
<p>In our case, the string decryption function is at <strong>0x42572C</strong> (0x431192 + 0xFFFF459A). Our next step is finding where the function is called; however, when we checked the <em>xrefs</em> for this function, we saw that it had no references.</p>
<p><img loading="lazy" decoding="async" class="size-full wp-image-120102 aligncenter" src="https://www.cyberark.com/wp-content/uploads/2021/07/7.png" alt="Figure 7: string_dercypt function has no references" width="630" height="173" srcset="https://www.cyberark.com/wp-content/uploads/2021/07/7.png 630w, https://www.cyberark.com/wp-content/uploads/2021/07/7-300x82.png 300w, https://www.cyberark.com/wp-content/uploads/2021/07/7-150x41.png 150w" sizes="auto, (max-width: 630px) 100vw, 630px"></p>
<p style="text-align: center;">Figure 7: string_dercypt function has no references</p>
<p>We believe there are two reasons for this:</p>
<ol>
<li>The malware calls this function dynamically in an obfuscated way, as we saw earlier, or</li>
<li>It has other string decryption routines (which is not likely.)</li>
</ol>
<p>Before digging into this issue, let&rsquo;s focus on the <span class="cyb-inline-code-labs">string_decrypt</span> function.</p>
<h4>String Decryption Function</h4>
<p>The string decryption function receives three arguments: a <strong>destination pointer to write the decrypted string</strong>, the <strong>string length</strong> and the <strong>pointer to the obfuscated string</strong>.</p>
<p>The obfuscated string has a pretty simple structure. The first four bytes are the <strong>key</strong> for the decryption algorithm, and the rest of the bytes (as the size of the string length argument) are the <strong>encrypted string</strong>.</p>
<p>After delving deeper into the function, we noticed a few things:</p>
<ol>
<li>The pointer for the obfuscated string is also obfuscated; the pointer is not the one for the &ldquo;real&rdquo; structure (that we described before).</li>
<li>The function first calculates the &ldquo;real&rdquo; address and retrieves the encrypted string and the decryption key.</li>
<li>After getting the &ldquo;real&rdquo; structure, the function implements the decryption algorithm.</li>
</ol>
<p>The function uses a hardcoded constant to calculate the address of the &ldquo;real&rdquo; string structure. As much as we know &mdash; and at the time of writing this blog post &mdash; this constant remains the same in all Ficker&rsquo;s samples. It&rsquo;s <strong>0x25</strong>.</p>
<p>The address of the structure is calculated in this way:<br>
<span class="cyb-inline-code-labs">enc_struc&nbsp;=&nbsp;obf_addr&nbsp;+&nbsp;string_dec_const&nbsp;*&nbsp;str_len&nbsp;&ndash;&nbsp;4</span>.<br>
For example, the function gets the address 0x436FF8 (<em>obf_addr</em>) and the length of the decrypted string which is 0x06 (<em>str_len</em>). Eventually, we&rsquo;ll get the address 0x437494 that holds the &ldquo;real&rdquo; string structure for Pidgin.</p>
<p>After getting the &ldquo;real&rdquo; address, the function decrypts the string using a simple decryption algorithm.</p>
<p>We wrote an IDAPython script that simulates the <span class="cyb-inline-code-labs">string_decrypt</span> &nbsp;function, including the decryption algorithm.</p>
<p><img loading="lazy" decoding="async" class="size-full wp-image-120110 aligncenter" src="https://www.cyberark.com/wp-content/uploads/2021/07/8.png" alt="Figure 8: IDAPython script for string_decrypt &nbsp;function" width="537" height="346" srcset="https://www.cyberark.com/wp-content/uploads/2021/07/8.png 537w, https://www.cyberark.com/wp-content/uploads/2021/07/8-300x193.png 300w, https://www.cyberark.com/wp-content/uploads/2021/07/8-150x97.png 150w" sizes="auto, (max-width: 537px) 100vw, 537px"></p>
<p style="text-align: center;">Figure 8: IDAPython script for <span class="cyb-inline-code-labs">string_decrypt</span> &nbsp;function</p>
<p>After writing a script that can decrypt any string, we just need to find where it&rsquo;s called and what the parameters are for each call (on every location).</p>
<p>It sounds like a great mission for an IDAPython script! If you remember, we noted that it is more likely that <span class="cyb-inline-code-labs">string_decrypt</span> will be called on runtime.</p>
<p>Let&rsquo;s break our mission into tasks:</p>
<ol>
<li>Find all dynamic calls in the binary &mdash; find all <span class="cyb-inline-code-labs">call</span> instructions with some register (e.g., <span class="cyb-inline-code-labs">call eax</span>).</li>
<li>Calculate the address in the register (can be obfuscated) and compare the calculated address to <span class="cyb-inline-code-labs">string_decrypt</span> function&rsquo;s address.</li>
<li>Get the function arguments&rsquo; values (string length, pointer to obfuscated string structure), which can also be obfuscated.</li>
<li>Use our IDAPython <span class="cyb-inline-code-labs">str_decrypt</span> function to decrypt the string (figure 8).</li>
</ol>
<p>The interesting part is how we can deal with Ficker&rsquo;s obfuscation. Ficker mostly uses obfuscation techniques like calculating the data, using useless instructions and pushing-popping data.</p>
<p>To deal with that, we wrote two IDAPython functions <span class="cyb-inline-code-labs">get_value</span>, <span class="cyb-inline-code-labs">get_ref</span>.</p>
<p>The primary function (<span class="cyb-inline-code-labs">get_value</span>) is a recursive function, which gets the instruction&rsquo;s address as an argument and returns the value used on this instruction, e.g., the address of <span class="cyb-inline-code-labs">push ebx</span> will return the value <span class="cyb-inline-code-labs">ebx</span> holds.</p>
<p>But a question comes to mind: How can we find it? We use the <span class="cyb-inline-code-labs">get_ref</span> function that gets this instruction&rsquo;s address and the operand index as arguments and returns the first reference to the given operand.</p>
<p>The <span class="cyb-inline-code-labs">get_value</span> function first identifies the instruction (<span class="cyb-inline-code-labs">call</span>, <span class="cyb-inline-code-labs">mov</span>, <span class="cyb-inline-code-labs">lea</span>, <span class="cyb-inline-code-labs">add</span> instruction, etc.&rsquo;). Therefore, we can tell that it knows which operand may contain the data and checks this operand&rsquo;s type if it is <span class="cyb-inline-code-labs">mem_addr</span> or <span class="cyb-inline-code-labs">imm_value</span>. These types of operand indicate if it&rsquo;s a piece of data that&rsquo;s stored as part of the instruction itself instead of being in a memory location or a register. In case the operand holds any value (<span class="cyb-inline-code-labs">mem_addr</span>/<span class="cyb-inline-code-labs">imm_value</span>), the function will return this value. Otherwise, the function gets a reference to this operand by calling <span class="cyb-inline-code-labs">get_ref</span> and then it calls recursively to itself with the new instruction&rsquo;s address. The recursive function breaks when it finds <span class="cyb-inline-code-labs">mem_addr</span>/<span class="cyb-inline-code-labs">imm_value</span>.</p>
<p>Let&rsquo;s see how it works with an example. The goal here is to find the address for the call on 0x415C6A:</p>
<pre class="enl" data-enlighter-language="raw">.text:00415C51                 lea     eax, loc_4342CA+2
.text:00415C57                 lea     ecx, unk_437BEF
.text:00415C5D                 mov     [edi], eax
.text:00415C5F                 mov     eax, 0FFFF1460h
.text:00415C64                 add     eax, [edi]
.text:00415C66                 push    ecx
.text:00415C67                 push    21h ; '!'
.text:00415C69                 push    esi
.text:00415C6A                 call    eax
</pre>
<p style="text-align: center;">Code Snippet 1: Disassembly snippet from Ficker&rsquo;s binary</p>
<p>We call our recursive function with the address 0x415C6A to find the address of <span class="cyb-inline-code-labs">eax</span>. The function checks the type of the relevant operand (in this case, the first one because it&rsquo;s a call instruction). The operand is a register, which is not a <span class="cyb-inline-code-labs">mem_addr</span>/<span class="cyb-inline-code-labs">imm_value</span>. Therefore, the function searches for a reference to this operand (<span class="cyb-inline-code-labs">eax</span>). The function calls itself with the new address of the reference, 0x415C64.</p>
<p>And again, it checks the type of both operands because it <span class="cyb-inline-code-labs">add</span>&nbsp;instruction and searches a reference for both operands (<span class="cyb-inline-code-labs">eax</span> and <span class="cyb-inline-code-labs">edi</span>). After the function finds those registers&rsquo; values recursively, it finally will return the sum of them.</p>
<h4>Ficker Deobfuscator: An IDAPython script</h4>
<p>You can find our IDAPython deobfuscator in our <a href="https://github.com/cyberark/malware-research/tree/master/FickerStealer/Ficker_deobfuscator" target="_blank" rel="noopener">Malware Research repository &mdash; FickerStealer</a>.</p>
<p>We gathered all those parts together and added more functionalities into one IDAPython script. The script defeats Ficker&rsquo;s obfuscation and decrypts all the strings along the binary that Ficker uses.</p>
<ol>
<li>The script finds the decryption function address by searching (using pattern-based) the string&rsquo;s decryption algorithm.</li>
<li>It obtains the addresses of all dynamic calls in the binary, and for each one, deobfuscate the address on the <span class="cyb-inline-code-labs">call</span> instruction and compares it to the decryption function address.</li>
<li>It gets the arguments (by stack) of the string decryption call; the string decryption function gets three arguments (destination pointer, string length and a pointer to the obfuscated string.)</li>
<li>It gets the data that the arguments should hold.</li>
<li>Deobfuscate the string using the function <span class="cyb-inline-code-labs">get_obf_string</span>, which gets the pointer for the obfuscated string and the string length.</li>
<li>This function returns the decrypted string.</li>
</ol>
<p><img loading="lazy" decoding="async" class="alignnone size-full wp-image-120118" src="https://www.cyberark.com/wp-content/uploads/2021/07/9.gif" alt="Figure 9: FickerStealer&rsquo;s deobfuscator usage" width="1920" height="1016"></p>
<p style="text-align: center;">Figure 9: FickerStealer&rsquo;s deobfuscator usage</p>
<p>The script also sets a reference to <span class="cyb-inline-code-labs">string_decrypt</span> function, adds a comment with the decrypted string and dumps all the data to a JSON file.</p>
<p>And now, having collected the fruits of our labor, our deobfuscator script was able to identify 191 calls to the string decryption function (string_decrypt) and to decrypt 182 strings in this sample.</p>
<p><img loading="lazy" decoding="async" class="size-full wp-image-120126 aligncenter" src="https://www.cyberark.com/wp-content/uploads/2021/07/10.png" alt="Figure 10: References for string_decrypt function" width="472" height="365" srcset="https://www.cyberark.com/wp-content/uploads/2021/07/10.png 472w, https://www.cyberark.com/wp-content/uploads/2021/07/10-300x232.png 300w, https://www.cyberark.com/wp-content/uploads/2021/07/10-150x116.png 150w" sizes="auto, (max-width: 472px) 100vw, 472px"></p>
<p style="text-align: center;">Figure 10: References for <span class="cyb-inline-code-labs">string_decrypt</span> function</p>
<p>Finally, after we got all Ficker&rsquo;s strings, we have an idea about what most functions and code parts do, so we can now move forward.</p>
<h3>C2 server communication</h3>
<h4>Establishing Connection</h4>
<p>Ficker holds the address and port for the C2 server as an encrypted string, <span class="cyb-inline-code-labs">IP:PORT</span>, which can be easily identified by running the IDAPython script. The string can be an IP address or domain name, and if the C2 address is a domain name, the malware tries to resolve it until it succeeds.</p>
<p>The malware communicates with the C2 server over TCP. It creates a TCP socket to the C2 server by using the IP address and the port. After the connection is established, Ficker calls to <span class="cyb-inline-code-labs">recv</span> function twice. First, it receives two bytes that indicate the data size it needs to allocate. It then allocates this memory and receives the data from the C2 server.</p>
<p>The received data is the Grabber rules configuration of the C2 server and contains a list of Grabber rules configured by the attacker. We will focus on this section on the Grabber part.</p>
<p>After getting the grabber rules sent as plaintext, Ficker sends some hardcoded value <span class="cyb-inline-code-labs">0x0C000F0A0B0A0B0A</span> (not encrypted) to the C2. We assume that this hardcoded value indicates that the connection was established successfully and the configuration has been received.</p>
<p><img loading="lazy" decoding="async" class="size-full wp-image-120134 aligncenter" src="https://www.cyberark.com/wp-content/uploads/2021/07/11.png" alt="Figure 11: Send the hardcoded value" width="276" height="107" srcset="https://www.cyberark.com/wp-content/uploads/2021/07/11.png 276w, https://www.cyberark.com/wp-content/uploads/2021/07/11-150x58.png 150w" sizes="auto, (max-width: 276px) 100vw, 276px"></p>
<p style="text-align: center;">Figure 11: Send the hardcoded value</p>
<p>The last part of this connection establishing method is sending the address of the C2 server encrypted/obfuscated using a function we named <span class="cyb-inline-code-labs">send_enc_data</span>.</p>
<h4>Sending Data to C2</h4>
<p>FickerStealer has a singular method for sending the data to the C2 server, and it does it in an encrypted/obfuscated way. Using this method makes the network analysis of this malware a little bit more difficult and stealthier than sending the data as plaintext like most of the stealers.</p>
<p>The function <span class="cyb-inline-code-labs">send_enc_data</span> gets two arguments: a <strong>pointer to the data</strong> and an <strong>index flag</strong>. The index flag indicates the data type used by the C2 server. This function first &ldquo;encrypts&rdquo; the data using simple XOR rotation with one byte XOR key: &nbsp;<span class="cyb-inline-code-labs">0x0A</span>. So far, we noticed the XOR byte is the same in all Ficker&rsquo;s samples.</p>
<p>After encrypting the data and before sending it, Ficker creates a packet for sending the data:</p>
<p><span style="padding-left: 50px;">4 bytes: the size of the encrypted data,</span><br>
<span style="padding-left: 50px;">1 byte: the index flag</span><br>
<span style="padding-left: 50px;">X bytes: the encrypted data</span></p>
<p>Finally, Ficker sends this data to the C2 server in <strong>two separate requests</strong>.</p>
<p><img loading="lazy" decoding="async" class="size-full wp-image-120142 aligncenter" src="https://www.cyberark.com/wp-content/uploads/2021/07/12.png" alt="Figure 12: The simple XOR rotation and sending the first packet" width="563" height="570" srcset="https://www.cyberark.com/wp-content/uploads/2021/07/12.png 563w, https://www.cyberark.com/wp-content/uploads/2021/07/12-296x300.png 296w, https://www.cyberark.com/wp-content/uploads/2021/07/12-150x152.png 150w, https://www.cyberark.com/wp-content/uploads/2021/07/12-100x100.png 100w" sizes="auto, (max-width: 563px) 100vw, 563px"></p>
<p style="text-align: center;">Figure 12: The simple XOR rotation and sending the first packet</p>
<p>As we mentioned earlier, the index flag indicates the data type sent to the C2 server. We check the xrefs of this function, and we found out it is called from 13 different locations and gets 12 different values of the index flag argument, from 0x00 to 0x0B.</p>
<p>By setting a conditional breakpoint on the entry of this function, we could dump the plaintext data and the index flag on every call. By correlating these parameters, we figured out what each index flag value represents.</p>
<table id="tableOne" class="table table-bordered table-striped table-responsive-stack" width="100%">
<thead>
<tr>
<td width="50%"><strong>Index Flag value</strong></td>
<td width="50%"><strong>Data type</strong></td>
</tr>
</thead>
<tbody>
<tr>
<td width="50%"><strong>0x00</strong></td>
<td width="50%">Chromium-based browsers</td>
</tr>
<tr>
<td width="50%"><strong>0x01</strong></td>
<td width="50%">Mozilla-based browsers</td>
</tr>
<tr>
<td width="50%"><strong>0x02</strong></td>
<td width="50%">FTP clients</td>
</tr>
<tr>
<td width="50%"><strong>0x03</strong></td>
<td width="50%">Grabber files</td>
</tr>
<tr>
<td width="50%"><strong>0x04</strong></td>
<td width="50%">Screenshots</td>
</tr>
<tr>
<td width="50%"><strong>0x05</strong></td>
<td width="50%">System information</td>
</tr>
<tr>
<td width="50%"><strong>0x06</strong></td>
<td width="50%">Cryptocurrency wallets</td>
</tr>
<tr>
<td width="50%"><strong>0x07</strong></td>
<td width="50%">Hardware ID</td>
</tr>
<tr>
<td width="50%"><strong>0x08</strong></td>
<td width="50%">Others (Desktop clients)</td>
</tr>
<tr>
<td width="50%"><strong>0x09</strong></td>
<td width="50%">C2 address and port</td>
</tr>
<tr>
<td width="50%"><strong>0x0A</strong></td>
<td width="50%"><span class="cyb-inline-code-labs">poppush</span> &ndash; Downloader command</td>
</tr>
<tr>
<td width="50%"><strong>0x0B</strong></td>
<td width="50%">Victim public IP</td>
</tr>
</tbody>
</table>
<p style="text-align: center;">Table 1: Mapped index flag values</p>
<h3>Stealing Capabilities</h3>
<p>Most applications store confidential information in various files/registry keys, and most of the information stealers have a hardcoded list of known/default locations where the sensitive file is located for each targeted application. Ficker&rsquo;s methodology of stealing sensitive information from files is quite different from other stealers.</p>
<p>Ficker doesn&rsquo;t have this typical path list for each application. It recursively searches a unique file that belongs to the targeted application from a starting location (path). This recursive search is implemented by a function we named <span class="cyb-inline-code-labs">find_file</span>. The function gets a path (starting location) and the sensitive file&rsquo;s name to search, which can also be a regex expression. The function returns a list of all the locations where the file was found under the starting location. This method allows Ficker to find the application folder containing the sensitive files without knowing exactly where they are stored (the application may be installed on a different location) &mdash; and without having all those suspicious strings.</p>
<h4>Mozilla-based applications</h4>
<p>First, Ficker calls to <span class="cyb-inline-code-labs">find_file</span> with the full path of <span class="cyb-inline-code-labs">%appdata%</span> and the file name <span class="cyb-inline-code-labs">cookies.sqlite</span> &mdash; a file used by Mozilla-based application that stores web cookies. By doing that, it gets a list of all profile folders for every installed Mozilla-based application. After it finds every sensitive folder, it moves to the next step: <strong>stealing the data</strong>. Ficker steals the user&rsquo;s <strong>saved login credentials, cookies, </strong>and<strong> autocomplete history</strong>. All those sensitive files are under the locations Ficker found at the first step.</p>
<p>The main difference for stealing Mozilla-based applications&rsquo; information is the way in which Ficker steals the user&rsquo;s saved credentials. Most of the stealers parse the <span class="cyb-inline-code-labs">login.json</span> file and decrypt the confidential information by getting the decryption key from other known database files (<span class="cyb-inline-code-labs">key4.db</span>, <span class="cyb-inline-code-labs">cert9.db</span>, or <span class="cyb-inline-code-labs">key3.db</span>, <span class="cyb-inline-code-labs">cert8.db</span> for older versions). Instead of parsing the credentials file and dealing with the decryption part, Ficker sends those files to the C2 server, and the server does all the parsing and decrypting work of these credentials. Our guess here is that Ficker does this to run without having any dependencies like <em>nss3.dll</em> and to appear less suspicious.</p>
<p>In the case of data that doesn&rsquo;t require decryption operations, such as the autocomplete and cookies data, Ficker parses it on the victim&rsquo;s side and then sends the data to the C2 server.</p>
<h4>Chromium-based browsers</h4>
<p>Ficker employs a similar methodology to steal Chromium-based data like it used to steal from Mozilla-based applications.</p>
<p>To begin with, Ficker decrypts five strings that will be starting locations: &nbsp;<span class="cyb-inline-code-labs">%appdata%</span>, <span class="cyb-inline-code-labs">%localappdata%</span>, <span class="cyb-inline-code-labs">%userprofile%\Desktop</span>, <span class="cyb-inline-code-labs">%userprofile%\Documents</span>, <span class="cyb-inline-code-labs">%USERPROFILE%\Local Settings\Application Data</span>. It also decrypts the string <span class="cyb-inline-code-labs">Login Data</span>, the DB file name used by Chromium-based browsers that stores saved credentials. By searching this file under those starting locations, Ficker gets all Chromium-based browsers users&rsquo; profile folders.</p>
<p><img loading="lazy" decoding="async" class="size-full wp-image-120150 aligncenter" src="https://www.cyberark.com/wp-content/uploads/2021/07/13.png" alt="Figure 13: The folder found by find_file" width="839" height="152" srcset="https://www.cyberark.com/wp-content/uploads/2021/07/13.png 839w, https://www.cyberark.com/wp-content/uploads/2021/07/13-300x54.png 300w, https://www.cyberark.com/wp-content/uploads/2021/07/13-768x139.png 768w, https://www.cyberark.com/wp-content/uploads/2021/07/13-150x27.png 150w" sizes="auto, (max-width: 839px) 100vw, 839px"></p>
<p style="text-align: center;">Figure 13: The folder found by <span class="cyb-inline-code-labs">find_file</span></p>
<p>For each user&rsquo;s profile folder, it extracts the user&rsquo;s master key from the <span class="cyb-inline-code-labs">Local State</span> file by parsing this JSON file and getting the value of <span class="cyb-inline-code-labs">encrypted_key</span>. This value is <em>Base64</em> encoded and encrypted using <em>DPAPI</em>; hence, Ficker decodes this value and decrypts it using <span class="cyb-inline-code-labs">CryptUnprotectData</span>. This key is used to decrypt confidential information that Chromium-based browsers save in their SQLite database files.</p>
<p>For each existing profile in the browser, Ficker targets the <span class="cyb-inline-code-labs">Login Data</span>, <span class="cyb-inline-code-labs">Cookies</span>, and <span class="cyb-inline-code-labs">Web Data</span> SQLite DB files in order to steal the user&rsquo;s <strong>saved login credentials, cookies, and autocomplete history</strong>.</p>
<p>One of the exciting things about SQLite database files is that Ficker<strong> parses those files and extracts the sensitive data independently</strong>. As we mentioned earlier, Ficker doesn&rsquo;t have any dependencies like most of the stealers, which download or drop some dlls like <em>sqlite3.dll</em> that make their job easier. It is common to see information stealers using <em>sqlite3.dll</em>&rsquo;s exported functions to extract sensitive data using SQL queries. For instance, getting the user&rsquo;s saved credentials from <span class="cyb-inline-code-labs">Login Data</span> using this query: <span class="cyb-inline-code-labs">SELECT origin_url, username_value, password_value FROM logins</span>.</p>
<p>However, Ficker doesn&rsquo;t use any SQL query to extract the sensitive data from the DB files. It goes over the <a href="https://www.sqlite.org/fileformat.html">SQLite3 database file format</a> and extracts the sensitive file by parsing the DB file.</p>
<h4>Cryptocurrency wallets</h4>
<p>Like most information stealers, Ficker also steals crypto wallets. It doesn&rsquo;t target every crypto wallet application, but it does target the more popular ones. For every wallet application, Ficker decrypts the application folder location and the sensitive file name (e.g., <span class="cyb-inline-code-labs">*.wallet</span>). After getting the location of those files using <span class="cyb-inline-code-labs">find_file</span>, Ficker reads those files into memory and sends all the data to the C2 server.</p>
<p>The targeted applications are Bitcoin, Jaxx, Exodus, Atomic, Electrum, Zcash, Bytecoin, Ethreum, Monero, Litecoin, and Dash.</p>
<h4>System information</h4>
<p>Ficker gathers information and takes a screenshot of the compromised machine. When it finishes collecting this information, it gathers all the data together and sends it to the C2 server.</p>
<p>Moreover, Ficker collects basic information about the machine like the location of the running executable, OS version, hardware and graphic information, system time zone and language. In addition, it lists all the running process and their PIDs, as well as the installed applications on the victim&rsquo;s machine.</p>
<p><img loading="lazy" decoding="async" class="size-full wp-image-120158 aligncenter" src="https://www.cyberark.com/wp-content/uploads/2021/07/14.png" alt="Figure 14: Collected system information" width="1010" height="705" srcset="https://www.cyberark.com/wp-content/uploads/2021/07/14.png 1010w, https://www.cyberark.com/wp-content/uploads/2021/07/14-300x209.png 300w, https://www.cyberark.com/wp-content/uploads/2021/07/14-768x536.png 768w, https://www.cyberark.com/wp-content/uploads/2021/07/14-150x105.png 150w" sizes="auto, (max-width: 1010px) 100vw, 1010px"></p>
<p style="text-align: center;">Figure 14: Collected system information</p>
<h3>Grabber</h3>
<p>FickerStealer has a grabber feature that allows the attacker to collect any files from the victim&rsquo;s machine and send those to the C2 server. This feature is configurable, and the attacker can enable or disable it from the C2 panel. When the malware establishes its connection to the C2 server (as we covered earlier), it gets the Grabber&rsquo;s configuration from the C2 server.</p>
<p>The received data contains a list of Grabber rules. Each rule has the start location and its mask; the mask can be a regex or file name, so the malware searches for files by the mask under the start location recursively.</p>
<p><img loading="lazy" decoding="async" class="size-full wp-image-120166 aligncenter" src="https://www.cyberark.com/wp-content/uploads/2021/07/15.png" alt="Figure 15: The received Grabber&rsquo;s configuration" width="710" height="229" srcset="https://www.cyberark.com/wp-content/uploads/2021/07/15.png 710w, https://www.cyberark.com/wp-content/uploads/2021/07/15-300x97.png 300w, https://www.cyberark.com/wp-content/uploads/2021/07/15-150x48.png 150w" sizes="auto, (max-width: 710px) 100vw, 710px"></p>
<p style="text-align: center;">Figure 15: The received Grabber&rsquo;s configuration</p>
<p>The structure of the Grabber configuration looks like Code Snippet 2:</p>
<pre class="enl" data-enlighter-language="raw">struct rule{
	int path_len;
	char* startFolder; #size of path_len
	int mask_len;
	char* mask; #size of mask_len
	char end_flag;
};

struct grabberRules{
	int rules_num;
	rule* rule_list; #size of rules_num
};

</pre>
<p style="text-align: center;">Code Snippet 2: Grabber configuration structure</p>
<p>We wrote a simple python script that parses this data by this structure. We noticed that attackers mainly focus on searching other cryptocurrency wallets in different locations, password manager confidential information and password files. For instance, <span class="cyb-inline-code-labs">path:%userprofile%\Documents</span>, <span class="cyb-inline-code-labs">mask:*.kdbx</span> targets KDBX files, which are used by KeePass and usually refer to the KeePass Password Database. You can check our IoCs page in our <a href="https://github.com/cyberark/malware-research/tree/master/FickerStealer" target="_blank" rel="noopener">Malware Research Repository</a> to find more examples of Grabber&rsquo;s rules.</p>
<h3>Downloader</h3>
<p>After Ficker steals as much data as possible, it moves for its last feature, which is the Loader. Ficker can download and execute several second-stage malware that can be done by running executables or loading DLLs.</p>
<p>The attacker can enable/disable the downloader feature from the C2 panel. The panel of the malware also allows setting checks prior to downloading the second stage malware &mdash; checking some of the victim&rsquo;s characteristics like GeoIP, stolen credentials type, etc.</p>
<p>Before receiving the second stage malware from the C2 server, Ficker sends a command to the C2 server- <span class="cyb-inline-code-labs">poppush</span>.</p>
<p>The malware first decrypts the string <span class="cyb-inline-code-labs">poppush</span> and then sends it to the C2 server by using <span class="cyb-inline-code-labs">send_enc_data</span> function. We assume that this string/magic commands the C2 server to return the second-stage malware data.</p>
<p><img loading="lazy" decoding="async" class="size-full wp-image-120174 aligncenter" src="https://www.cyberark.com/wp-content/uploads/2021/07/16.png" alt="Figure 16: Downloader communication" width="743" height="253" srcset="https://www.cyberark.com/wp-content/uploads/2021/07/16.png 743w, https://www.cyberark.com/wp-content/uploads/2021/07/16-300x102.png 300w, https://www.cyberark.com/wp-content/uploads/2021/07/16-150x51.png 150w" sizes="auto, (max-width: 743px) 100vw, 743px"></p>
<p style="text-align: center;">Figure 16: Downloader communication</p>
<p>As we can see in figure 16, the malware sends the XORred <span class="cyb-inline-code-labs">poppush</span> string, and it receives a data structure from the C2 server.</p>
<p>The first four bytes are the data size, so Ficker first receives only four bytes and then allocates a new memory buffer for the Downloader data. The next four bytes indicate the number of binaries that received from the C2 server. In the communication with the C2 server above, we can see it&rsquo;s configured to download two binaries. The next four bytes indicate the first binary size, which is right after these four bytes.</p>
<p>After receiving the second-stage malware, Ficker generates a random name for each binary and gets the Temp folder of the current user. Ficker decrypts the binary&rsquo;s extension string, which can be <span class="cyb-inline-code-labs">.exe</span> or <span class="cyb-inline-code-labs">.dll</span> depending on the binary type. Then it concatenates all those three strings into a valid file path &ndash; <span class="cyb-inline-code-labs">%temp%+random+decrypted_exetension</span>, e.g., <em>C:\Users\win10\AppData\Local\Temp\1606398000688.exe</em>. After that, Ficker writes the binary data to this path.</p>
<p>The last part of this feature is executing the second stage malware. Ficker uses <span class="cyb-inline-code-labs">CreateProcess</span> to run an executable, and it uses <span class="cyb-inline-code-labs">LoadLibraryA</span> to load a DLL.</p>
<h2>Conclusion</h2>
<p>We foresee an expanding number of opportunities for FickerStealer among cybercriminals because of its efficiency and differentiation from other stealers, such as its stealing methodology and being written in Rust.</p>
<p>Ficker has all the capabilities that cybercriminals look for in information stealers. Moreover, Ficker uses different obfuscation techniques and because it&rsquo;s written in Rust, it makes the analysis of the malware more difficult &mdash; leading us to one of our main conclusions. Our first strategy to &ldquo;map&rdquo; the malware by finding its strings paid off because it saved us time analyzing the sample. By doing that, we could eventually understand any part of the malware quite quickly and focus only on the features that interest us without digging into the malware.</p>
<p>Credential-theft malware will always be a popular resource used by adversaries. Even though information stealers might not be the most sophisticated malware, they are still effective enough to cause significant damage to organizations and individuals.</p>
<p>To defend against these threats, organizations can use an endpoint protection solution like CyberArk Endpoint Privilege Manager, combine it with fundamental security awareness, deploy MFA and use of strong and unique passwords to further mitigate risk.</p>

    /* Get uuid - used to pass back to bombora as our main identifier of this visitor */
    window.getBomboraUuid = function() {
        return '';
    };

    /* Get page id - used to pass back to bombora as the item being viewed */
    window.getBomboraUrlId = function(){
        var env = 'production';
        var $infoDiv = $('#page-type-identifier');
        var item = {
          type : $infoDiv.attr('data-page-type'),
          collectionId : $infoDiv.attr('data-collection-id'),
          id : $infoDiv.attr('data-item-id')
        };

        if(item.type === 'PAGE_TYPE_HUB'){
            return env + '||' + Hubs.Config.hubId;
        } else if(item.type === 'PAGE_TYPE_COLLECTION'){
            return env + '||' + Hubs.Config.hubId + '||' + item.collectionId;
        } else if(item.type === 'PAGE_TYPE_ITEM'){
            return env + '||' + Hubs.Config.hubId + '||' + item.collectionId + '||' + item.id;
        }
    };

<script nonce="q9yaXe5caxU4VZynlW784LjY">
var getMatchingTileTag = function(elementTags, tagList) {
  
    for (var tag in tagList) {
      var normalizedTags = elementTags.toLowerCase().split(',');
      var regEx = new RegExp(tag, 'gi');
    
      if (regEx.test(normalizedTags)) {
          return tag;
      }
    }

};

var embedFixes = function() {
     var frontEndTags = window.obData.frontEndTags;
    
    if(document.getElementById("embed-tile") === null && document.querySelector(".embedTileImg")) {
        var embedTileImg = document.querySelector(".embedTileImg");
        embedTileImg.parentNode.id = "embed-tile";
    }
    var embedTile = document.getElementById("embed-tile");
    if(embedTile !== null){
        document.querySelector("body").classList.add("embed-tile-present");
    }
    
     if(document.querySelector('.embed-tile-present')){
        var tags = document.querySelector('#embed-tile').dataset.tags;
        var label = getMatchingTileTag(tags, frontEndTags);
        document.querySelector('.h3like').insertAdjacentHTML('beforebegin', '<p class="ob-custom-label">' + label + '</p>');
        }
};
embedFixes();
}(window.jQuery, window.Hubs));
</script>

// Asset Information

var ufPageTitle =  $("meta[name='title']").attr("content");
    
// Define asset type based on Item tag
    var prefix = $('#page-type-identifier').attr("data-tags");
    if ($('body').hasClass('single-page') && prefix !== null && prefix !== undefined){
    
    // Convert tag list into array
    var tagArray = prefix.split(',');

    if (tagArray.includes('White paper')) {
        var ufAssetType = 'White Paper';
    } else if (tagArray.includes('Infographic')) {
        var ufAssetType = 'Infographic';
    } else if (tagArray.includes('EBook')) {
        var ufAssetType = 'EBook';
    } else if (tagArray.includes('Product Datasheet')) {
        var ufAssetType = 'Product Datasheet';
    } else if (tagArray.includes('Solution Brief')) {
        var ufAssetType = 'Solution Brief';
    } else if (tagArray.includes('Video')) {
        var ufAssetType = 'Video';
    } else if (tagArray.includes('Case Study')) {
        var ufAssetType = 'Case Study';
    } else if (tagArray.includes('Webinar')) {
        var ufAssetType = 'Webinar';

// First Submission Data Layer Push - a Form CTA is physically filled out and submitted
function firstSubmission(ctaId, ctaData, ctaName){
    window.dataLayer = window.dataLayer || [];
    
    if (ctaId == 312282 || ctaId == 319542){ // Asset download
        window.dataLayer.push({        
           'event' : 'asset_download',  // trigger
           'asset_type': ufAssetType, 
           'download_type': 'Gated Download', 
           'asset_title': ufPageTitle
        });
    } else if (ctaId == 314328){ // Demo Request
        window.dataLayer.push({        
           'event' : 'demo',
           'form_submit': 'Demo'
        });
    } else if (ctaId == 314334){ // Contact Us
        window.dataLayer.push({        
           'event' : 'contact_us',  
           'form_submit': 'Contact Us'
        });
    } else if (ctaId == 314340){ // DNA CTA
        window.dataLayer.push({        
           'event' : 'dna',  
           'form_submit': 'DNA'
        });
    } else if (ctaId == 314343){ // EPM CTA
        window.dataLayer.push({        
           'event' : 'epm_free_trial',  
           'form_submit': 'EPM Free Trial'
        });
    } else if (ctaId == 315201){ // Blueprint CTA
        window.dataLayer.push({        
           'event' : 'blueprint',  
           'form_submit': 'CyberArk Blueprint'
        });
    } else if (ctaId == 315177){ // CyberArk Guided Tour CTA
        window.dataLayer.push({        
           'event' : 'guided_tour',  
           'form_submit': 'CyberArk Guided Tour'
        });
    } else if (ctaId == 315153){ // Alero Free Trial
        window.dataLayer.push({        
           'event' : 'alero_free_trial',  
           'form_submit': 'Alero Free Trial'
        });
    } else if (ctaId == 315189){ // Alero Guided Tour
        window.dataLayer.push({        
           'event' : 'alero_guided_tour',  
           'form_submit': 'Alero Guided Tour'
        });
    } else if (ctaId == 315219){ // Privilege Cloud Demo
        window.dataLayer.push({        
           'event' : 'privilege_cloud_demo',  
           'form_submit': 'Privilege Cloud Demo'
        });
    }
}

// Known User - Gated Asset and Non-Gated Assets
function secondarySubmission() {
        
    window.dataLayer = window.dataLayer || [];
    
    // Secondary submission, no gate visible since user is fully profiled
    if (window.localStorage.getItem('flyptech-hub-108540') !== null && !$('div').hasClass('blocking-cta') && $('div.block-cta').children().length > 0) {
        window.dataLayer.push({    
           'event' : 'asset_download',  // trigger
           'asset_type': ufAssetType, // Category
           'download_type': 'Gated Download', // Action
           'asset_title': ufPageTitle // Label
        });
        // console.log('Gated asset viewed, no CTA');
        // console.log(window.dataLayer);
    }
        
    // Ungated asset is viewed, excluding blog post Item types
    if ($('body').hasClass('single-page') && !$('div').hasClass('blocking-cta') && Hubs.appInstance.itemType !== 'blogpost' && $('div.block-cta').children().length === 0) {
        window.dataLayer.push({
           'event' : 'asset_download',  // trigger
           'asset_type': ufAssetType, // Category
           'download_type': 'Ungated Download', // Action
           'asset_title': ufPageTitle // Label
        });
        // console.log('Ungated asset was viewed');
        // console.log(window.dataLayer);
    }   
}

  <script nonce="q9yaXe5caxU4VZynlW784LjY">

function persistParams() {

    urlArray = document.referrer.split('?'); // Split out the referrer's params...
    if (urlArray[1]) {
    var oldParams = new URLSearchParams( urlArray[1] ); 
    var myURL = new URL(window.location.href); // The existing URL
    
    // console.log('referrer:' + document.referrer);
    // console.log(urlArray);
    
    if (oldParams.has("utm_medium")) {
    myURL.searchParams.set('utm_medium', oldParams.get("utm_medium")); // more direct solution
    }
    if (oldParams.has("utm_source")) {
    myURL.searchParams.set('utm_source', oldParams.get("utm_source"));
    }
    if (oldParams.has("utm_campaign")) {
    myURL.searchParams.set('utm_campaign', oldParams.get("utm_campaign"));
    }
    if (oldParams.has("utm_content")) {
    myURL.searchParams.set('utm_content', oldParams.get("utm_content"));
    }
    if (oldParams.has("utm_term")) {
    myURL.searchParams.set('utm_term', oldParams.get("utm_term"));
    }
    window.history.replaceState( {} , 'Refresh UTM params', myURL.toString() ); 
    }
}

persistParams();

function getQueryString() {
    var query_string = {};
    var query = window.location.search.substring(1);
    var vars = query.split("&");
        for (var i=0;i<vars.length;i++) {
            var pair = vars[i].split("=");
            if (typeof query_string[pair[0]] === "undefined") {
                query_string[pair[0]] = pair[1];
            } else if (typeof query_string[pair[0]] === "string") {
                var arr = [ query_string[pair[0]], pair[1] ];
                query_string[pair[0]] = arr;
            } else {
                query_string[pair[0]].push(pair[1]);
            }
        } 
    return query_string;
}

var queryString = getQueryString();

function submitQueryStrings(){
    if (queryString.utm_campaign !== undefined){
        $('.cta.setValues div.hidden-cta-fields .cta-field-section [data-mapping="utmcampaign"]').val(queryString.utm_campaign);
    }
    if (queryString.utm_medium !== undefined){
        $('.cta.setValues div.hidden-cta-fields .cta-field-section [data-mapping="utmmedium"]').val(queryString.utm_medium);
    }
    if (queryString.utm_source !== undefined){
        $('.cta.setValues div.hidden-cta-fields .cta-field-section [data-mapping="utmsource"]').val(queryString.utm_source);
    }
    if (queryString.utm_content !== undefined){
        $('.cta.setValues div.hidden-cta-fields .cta-field-section [data-mapping="utmcontent"]').val(queryString.utm_content);
    }
    if (queryString.utm_term !== undefined){
        $('.cta.setValues div.hidden-cta-fields .cta-field-section [data-mapping="utmterm"]').val(queryString.utm_term);
    }
}

Hubs.Events.on('ctaActivate', function(ctaId) {
    submitQueryStrings();
    
});

</script>

function stateControl(ctaId){
    var countryArray = ['Canada','United States','Australia'];
    var usArray = ['AK','AL','AR','AS','AZ','CA','CO','CT','DC','DE','FL','GA','GU','HI','IA','ID','IL','IN','KS','KY','LA','MA','MD','ME','MI','MN','MO','MP','MS','MT','NC','ND','NE','NH','NJ','NM','NV','NY','OH','OK','OR','PA','PR','RI','SC','SD','TN','TX','UM','UT','VA','VI','VT','WA','WI','WV','WY'];
    var canArray = ['AB','BC','MB','NB','NL','NT','NS','NU','ON','PE','QC','SK','YT'];
    var ausArray = ['Ashmore and Cartier Islands','Australian Antarctic Territory','Australian Capital Territory','Christmas Island','Cocos (Keeling) Islands','Coral Sea Islands','Heard Island and McDonald Islands','Jervis Bay Territory','New South Wales','Norfolk Island','Northern Territory','Queensland','South Australia','Tasmania','Victoria','Western Australia'];
    
    var checkbox = '<div class="cta-field-section one-line optin-container">' +
                    '<input name="consent" type="checkbox" class="preview-form-field optin-check">' +
                    '<span class="cta-field-name custom-cta">I’d like to receive educational material, news and insights from CyberArk and Venafi via email. I can revoke CyberArk and/or Venafi consent at any time.</span>' +
                    '</div>';
                    
    var privacyMsg = '<div class="cta-field-section">' +
                    '<span class="cta-field-name custom-cta privacy-msg">View <a href="https://www.cyberark.com/privacy-policy/" target="_blank">CyberArk’s Privacy Policy</a> and <a href="https://venafi.com/privacy/" target="_blank">Venafi Privacy Policy</a> for more information.</span>' +
                    '</div>';
                    
    var highValueMsg = '<div class="cta-field-section">' +
                    '<span class="cta-field-name custom-cta high-value-msg">Upon submitting this form, we will contact you using the information provided to assist you with your request.</span>' +
                    '</div>';
                    
    $('.cta.setValues div.hidden-cta-fields .cta-field-section').last().after(checkbox);
    $('.cta.setValues div.hidden-cta-fields .cta-field-section').last().after(privacyMsg);
    console.log('checkbox added');
    
    if (ctaId == 314334 || ctaId == 314328 || ctaId == 314340 || ctaId == 314343 || ctaId == 315153 || ctaId == 315177 || ctaId == 315189 || ctaId == 315201 || ctaId == 315219){
        $('.cta.setValues div.hidden-cta-fields .cta-field-section').last().after(highValueMsg);
    }
    
    var consentValue = $.cookie("ufconsent");
    
    if (consentValue === "true"){
        $('.optin-check').prop('checked', true);
    }
    
    var checkDropDown = function(){
        var countrySelected = $('.cta.setValues div.hidden-cta-fields .cta-field-section [data-mapping="country"] :selected').text();
        var attr = $('.cta.setValues div.hidden-cta-fields .cta-field-section [data-mapping="country"]').attr('data-value');
        
        // US behavior
        if(countrySelected.includes('United States')){
            console.log('US Selected');
    
            if (typeof attr !== typeof undefined && attr !== false && $('body').hasClass('single-page')){
                $('.cta.setValues div.hidden-cta-fields .cta-field-section [data-mapping="state"]').parent().hide();
                $('.cta.setValues div.hidden-cta-fields .cta-field-section [data-mapping="postalCode"]').parent().hide();
            } else {
                $('.cta.setValues div.hidden-cta-fields .cta-field-section [data-mapping="state"]').parent().show();
                $('.cta.setValues div.hidden-cta-fields .cta-field-section [data-mapping="postalCode"]').parent().show();
            }
            $('.cta.setValues div.hidden-cta-fields .cta-field-section [data-mapping="Opt_in__c"]').parent().detach();
            $('.optin-check').parent().hide();
            
            $(".cta.setValues div.hidden-cta-fields .cta-field-section [data-mapping='state'] > option").each(function() {
                $( this ).hide();
                if(jQuery.inArray(this.value, usArray) !== -1){
                    $( this ).show();
                }
            });
        
        // Canada behavior
        }else if(countrySelected.includes('Canada')){
            console.log('Canada Selected');
            
            if (typeof attr !== typeof undefined && attr !== false && $('body').hasClass('single-page')){
                $('.cta.setValues div.hidden-cta-fields .cta-field-section [data-mapping="state"]').parent().hide();
                $('.cta.setValues div.hidden-cta-fields .cta-field-section [data-mapping="postalCode"]').parent().hide();
            } else {
                $('.cta.setValues div.hidden-cta-fields .cta-field-section [data-mapping="state"]').parent().show();
                $('.cta.setValues div.hidden-cta-fields .cta-field-section [data-mapping="postalCode"]').parent().hide();
            }
            $('.cta.setValues div.hidden-cta-fields .cta-field-section [data-mapping="Opt_in__c"]').parent().show();
            $('.optin-check').parent().show();
            
            $(".cta.setValues div.hidden-cta-fields .cta-field-section [data-mapping='state'] > option").each(function() {
                
                $( this ).hide();
                if(jQuery.inArray(this.value, canArray) !== -1){
                    $( this ).show();
                }
            });
        
        // Australia behavior
        } else if (countrySelected.includes('Australia')){
            console.log('Australia Selected');
            if (typeof attr !== typeof undefined && attr !== false && $('body').hasClass('single-page')){
                $('.cta.setValues div.hidden-cta-fields .cta-field-section [data-mapping="state"]').parent().hide();
                $('.cta.setValues div.hidden-cta-fields .cta-field-section [data-mapping="postalCode"]').parent().hide();
            } else {
                $('.cta.setValues div.hidden-cta-fields .cta-field-section [data-mapping="state"]').parent().show();
                $('.cta.setValues div.hidden-cta-fields .cta-field-section [data-mapping="postalCode"]').parent().hide();
            }
            $('.cta.setValues div.hidden-cta-fields .cta-field-section [data-mapping="Opt_in__c"]').parent().show();
            $('.cta.setValues div.hidden-cta-fields .optin-check').parent().show();
            
            $(".cta.setValues div.hidden-cta-fields .cta-field-section [data-mapping='state'] > option").each(function() {
                
                $( this ).hide();
                if(jQuery.inArray(this.value, ausArray) !== -1){
                    $( this ).show();
                }
            });
            
        // Other behavior
        } else {
            $('.cta.setValues div.hidden-cta-fields .cta-field-section [data-mapping="state"]').parent().hide();
            $('.cta.setValues div.hidden-cta-fields .cta-field-section [data-mapping="postalCode"]').parent().hide();
            $('.cta.setValues div.hidden-cta-fields .cta-field-section [data-mapping="Opt_in__c"]').parent().show();
            $('.cta.setValues div.hidden-cta-fields .optin-check').parent().show();
        }
    };
    
    var checkConsent = function(){
    var countrySelected = $('.cta.setValues div.hidden-cta-fields .cta-field-section [data-mapping="country"] :selected').text();
    if(!countrySelected.includes('United States')){
            var consentGiven = $('.optin-check').is(':checked');
            if (consentGiven === true) {
                $('[data-mapping=Opt_in__c]').val('Yes');
                $('[data-mapping=Opt_in__c]').attr('data-value', 'Yes');
                $.cookie("ufconsent", "true", { expires: 365 });
            } else {
                $('[data-mapping=Opt_in__c]').val('No');
                $('[data-mapping=Opt_in__c]').attr('data-value', 'No');
                $.cookie("ufconsent", "false", { expires: 365 });
            }
        }
    if(countrySelected.includes('United States')){
        $('[data-mapping="Opt_in__c"]').val('');
        $('[data-mapping="Opt_in__c"]').attr('data-value', '');
    }
    };
    
    //On Change functions
    $(document).on('change','.cta.setValues div.hidden-cta-fields .cta-field-section [data-mapping="country"]',function(){ 
        checkDropDown();
    });
    $('.optin-check').change(function(){ checkConsent(); });
    //Init listener functions
    checkDropDown();
    checkConsent();
}

// Change dummy field label on pre-activate state
function previewField() {
    var halfProfile = $('div.hidden-cta-fields .cta-field-section [data-mapping="country"]').attr('data-value');
    var fullProfile = $('div.hidden-cta-fields .cta-field-section [data-mapping="phone"]').attr('data-value');
    
    if (typeof halfProfile !== typeof undefined && halfProfile !== false && $('body').hasClass('single-page') && typeof fullProfile == typeof undefined){
        $('.cta-activate-button-container .cta-field-section span.cta-field-name').text('Phone Number');
    } else if (typeof fullProfile !== typeof undefined && fullProfile !== false && $('body').hasClass('single-page')){
        $('.cta-activate-button-container .cta-field-section span.cta-field-name').text('Email');
    }
}

// Ensure email, privacy and checkbox show at a minimum for fully profiled users, seeing a never hide gated CTA
function thirdPartyCTA(ctaId){
    var fullProfile = $('div.hidden-cta-fields .cta-field-section [data-mapping="phone"]').attr('data-value');
    
    if (ctaId == 319542 && typeof fullProfile !== typeof undefined && fullProfile !== false && $('body').hasClass('single-page')){
        $('.cta.setValues div.hidden-cta-fields .cta-field-section [data-mapping="email"]').parent().show();   
    }
}

// Open up 3rd party CTA links when Form CTA is submitted
function thirdPartyCtaLink(){
    var ctaData = $('body').find('.cta').data();
    console.log(ctaData.ctaId);
    
    if (ctaData.ctaId == 319542){

    var link = $('.cta-button-container a.cta-button').attr('href');
    
         Hubs.Events.on('ctaFormSubmitSuccess', () => {
            window.open(link, '_blank');
            $('.blocking-cta').css('opacity', '0.2');
            $('.blocking-cta').css('filter', 'blur(4px)');
            $('.blocking-cta').css('-webkit-filter', 'blur(4px)');
            $('.fullscreen-controls').hide();
            
            setTimeout(function(){ 
                $('.single-embed-wrapper .block-cta').show();
            }, 3000);    
        });
        
        // $('div.form-fields .cta-button-container .cta-submit-form').on( "click", function(e) {
        //     window.open(link, '_blank');
        //     $('.blocking-cta').css('opacity', '0.2');
        //     $('.blocking-cta').css('filter', 'blur(4px)');
        //     $('.blocking-cta').css('-webkit-filter', 'blur(4px)');
        //     $('.fullscreen-controls').hide();
            
        //     setTimeout(function(){ 
        //         $('.single-embed-wrapper .block-cta').show();
        //     }, 3000);
        // });
    }
}

// Hub Events
Hubs.Events.on('ctaActivate', function(ctaId) {
    stateControl(ctaId);
    thirdPartyCTA(ctaId);
    thirdPartyCtaLink();
});

Hubs.Events.on('load', function() {
    previewField();
});

    });
        
    flexTable = function() {
        if (jQuery(window).width() < 768) {
            jQuery(".table-responsive-stack").each(function (i) {
                jQuery(this).find(".table-responsive-stack-thead").show();
                jQuery(this).find('thead').hide();
            });
            
         // window is less than 768px   
         } else {      
            jQuery(".table-responsive-stack").each(function (i) {
               jQuery(this).find(".table-responsive-stack-thead").hide();
               jQuery(this).find('thead').show();
            });
        }
    // flextable   
    };      
      
    flexTable();      
    window.onresize = function(event) {
        flexTable();
    }; 
}
</script>
